               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202300135-sec-0090">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202300135-sec-0090-title">3.1 Understanding the Na deposition in Na metal anode with Alloy Interface Layer</h3>
            
            <p>A constant amount of Na was galvanostatically deposited (2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> at 2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) onto the pristine Na anode and Na anode with alloy interface to probe the sodium deposition and dendrite formation process. The SEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) shows that Na plating is highly inhomogeneous with agglomerated and rough deposits over pristine Na metal. Such rough Na deposits over pristine Na metal is occurred due to inhomogeneous local current distribution that provoke non-uniform Na<sup>+</sup> ion flux.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0031_R_d62996621e953" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Eng. J.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na<sup>+</sup> ion seeding process in pristine Na metal has to overcome higher nucleation energy barrier, provoking the dendrite deposition in host-less Na metal. Such uneven Na deposition will induce severe side reactions between Na metal and the electrolyte, decreasing the coulombic efficiency of the cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0032_R_d62996621e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the Na deposition over Na metal with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy shows the uniform, and smoother deposition without formation of dendrites on the surface (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0002">2a</a>). The smoother Na deposition without dendrites will reduce the unwanted electrolyte reaction with Na metal, leading to increased cycle life of the alloy protected&nbsp;metal anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0033_R_d62996621e977" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>The cross-sectional SEM images of Na anode with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> layer indicates the Na deposition (bright layer) is beneath the Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy layer, and the thickness of the deposited Na metal is ≈10&nbsp;µm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0002">2b,c</a>). The top Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy layer is generally comprised of several Na-rich organic and inorganic SEI components with higher electron densities than Na metal, the top dark layer in the cross-sectional SEM corresponds to the Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy layer, whereas the bottom bright layer represents the Na deposits. The Na deposition below the Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy layer is mainly attributed to difference in the sodio-philicity and insulative properties of the hybrid alloy layer, and the SEI components over the metal anode. The Na-M alloy phases are likely to be electronic conductors or semiconductors but the NaCl formed as byproduct over Na-M alloy phase is highly insulating, making the hybrid artificial SEI layer highly resistive.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0003_R_d62996621e1007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Mater. Chem. A">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such highly resistance layer were previously studied in several protective layers and were even explored in some amorphous carbons and graphene based protective layers. The protective alloy layer possesses the lowest activation barrier and can facilitate a high potential gradient and rapid diffusion of sodium ions across the interphases. This unique feature of the hybrid SEI layer is sufficient enough to develop an electric field across the protective film, providing a driving force for Na-ion diffuse through the artificial SEI layer, and induces Na plating underneath the Na-M alloy interface layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0034_R_d62996621e1013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0035_R_d62996621e1013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Materialia">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202300135-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d6cd60c4-3876-46c7-a359-8c30f30a905b/adfm202300135-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d6cd60c4-3876-46c7-a359-8c30f30a905b/adfm202300135-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0de98324-4cbf-49f2-8739-6357d859ef9f/adfm202300135-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d6cd60c4-3876-46c7-a359-8c30f30a905b/adfm202300135-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202300135-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202300135" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–c) Cross-sectional SEM images of Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> deposited with 2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> at 2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> of Na, and SEM images of Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> with different Na deposition conditions d–f): 5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> at 2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, g–i) 10&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> at 2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and j–l) 2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> at 5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The Na anode with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy layer maintained a smoother Na deposition without dendrites even at high Na deposition capacity (5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>). SEM images in (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0002">2d–f</a>) shows that the Na metal with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy layer efficiently handled the Na deposition without any deleterious volume changes due to less reactivity of the Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> layer with electrolyte due to their lower chemical potential.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0036_R_d62996621e1084" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high mechanical strength of the alloy layer with hybrid SEI components can efficiently withstand volume expansion during high Na deposition process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0037_R_d62996621e1090" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>At higher Na deposition capacity (10&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>), a slight breakage of the Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> protective alloy layer is noted and Na deposition takes over the alloy layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0002">2g–i</a>). This is attributed to the inability of the thin alloy layer to infiltrate and holds more Na deposition at high capacity. However, no mossy and inhomogeneous dendrite deposits are noted over Na anode protected with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy even under such high capacity deposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0038_R_d62996621e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Small">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Even at high current density (5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) Na anode protected with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy showed smooth Na plating and stripping due reduction in effective current density, suppressing the dendritic growth (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0002">2j–l</a>). In contrast, pristine Na metal showed significant damages, the size of the dendrites and surface roughness of deposited Na metal increased over pristine Na anode at high current and high capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The dendrites grow vertically over pristine Na anode due to irregular amorphous grains that maximize the local current density. Such large Na dendrites formation over Na metal is highly dangerous, as it could puncture the separator, provoking the internal short circuit.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0039_R_d62996621e1127" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Acc. Chem. Res.">39</a>, <a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0040_R_d62996621e1127" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Joule">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The SEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) of Na anode with other alloy layers (Na<sub>1</sub>Bi<sub>1</sub>, Na<sub>1</sub>Zn<sub>13</sub>, and Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>2</sub>) showed a smoother Na deposition without dendrites at low deposition capacity and current density (2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, 2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>), which clearly explains that the combination of multi-interface layer benefits to the fast sodium diffusion and smoother deposition.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202300135-sec-0100">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202300135-sec-0100-title">3.2 Electrochemical Study of Na Metal Anodes</h3>
            
            <p>To verify the effectiveness of the protective alloy layer on Na metal, the pristine Na metal, and alloy protective Na metal were cycled in the symmetrical cell configuration at an aerial capacity of 2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and a current density of 2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0003">3a</a>). The voltage profile of pristine Na anode shows high overpotential (63&nbsp;mV) than Na anode with protective alloy, and the overpotential gradually increases upon cycling (74&nbsp;mV after 120&nbsp;h), followed by rigorous voltage fluctuations and the cell failure. The continuous increase in polarization of pristine Na anode with cycling is mainly attributed to unstable interface and parasitic reactions between Na metal and the electrolyte, leading to continuous breakage/formation of the SEI layer. The voltage fluctuations are associated with sudden cell failure, originating from Na dendrite penetration and short-circuiting.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0011_R_d62996621e1172" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Cent. Sci.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, Na metal anode with alloy protective layer shows a very stable voltage profile without any fluctuations. Na anode with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy layer showed the lowest overpotential of 11&nbsp;mV and could cycle for more than 790&nbsp;h without short-circuit. These electrochemical tests indicate that the protective alloy interface over Na anode increased the stability of the SEI layer during the repeated Na plating/stripping process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0024_R_d62996621e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>1</sub>Zn<sub>13</sub>, Na<sub>1</sub>Bi<sub>1</sub> protected Na anode can also be cycled efficiently with low overpotential, and polarization. The surface energy and diffusion barrier required for the Na<sup>+</sup> ion to diffusion reduces greatly, after the introduction of alloy layer, increasing the efficiency of Na anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0041_R_d62996621e1203" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202300135-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c3af1f1a-7ff4-4581-9e43-dfc4c1adae0f/adfm202300135-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c3af1f1a-7ff4-4581-9e43-dfc4c1adae0f/adfm202300135-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6b19b631-73e4-4e31-8388-b5efcd860119/adfm202300135-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c3af1f1a-7ff4-4581-9e43-dfc4c1adae0f/adfm202300135-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202300135-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202300135" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of symmetric cells with Na anode and alloy protected Na anode at 2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and capacity of 2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>: a) Voltage vs. time profile comparison, and b–e) Voltage vs. time profiles comparison at difference time range.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>However, the cycling efficiency and overpotential of the different alloys varies significantly after 300&nbsp;h. The cycle life of alloy protected Na anode increases in the following order (Na-Bi&lt; Na-Zn&lt;Na-Sn&lt;Na-In) and can be efficiently cycled over 365&nbsp;h, 446&nbsp;h, 618&nbsp;h, and 790&nbsp;h respectively. Moreover, voltage profiles are zoomed-in and presented in (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0003">3b–e</a>), and it is clearly evident from the voltage profiles, no fluctuation in voltage was observed for alloy protected Na anode even after prolonged cycling. This is mainly attributed to differentness in mechanical properties Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion co-efficient exhibited by the alloy layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0020_R_d62996621e1239" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The hybrid multifunctional interfaces are mechanically strong, due to the contribution from organic/inorganic layers with high young's modulus, thereby reducing the dendrite formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0042_R_d62996621e1244" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Electrochim. Acta">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The overpotential of Na anode with Na-In, Na-Sn, Na-Zn, and Na-Bi is 14&nbsp;mV, 21&nbsp;mV, 24&nbsp;mV, and 27&nbsp;mV respectively after 300 cycles. Moreover, the surface energy and diffusion barrier required for the Na<sup>+</sup> ion to diffuse across the surface of interlayer also influence the dendrite formation (discussed later in DFT). The Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy has the lowest surface energy than Na<sub>1</sub>Bi<sub>1</sub>, resulting in higher efficiency than other counterparts.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0031_R_d62996621e1261" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Eng. J.">31</a>, <a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0043_R_d62996621e1261" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Chem. Eng. J.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The thickness of the protective alloy layer on Na metal also influences the electrochemical performance of the metal anode. Therefore, the dependence of the long-term cyclability on the thickness of the Na-In layer is also studied. The thickness of the alloy layer over Na metal can be controlled by treating the Na metal anode with different concentration of the metal chloride solution. Subsequently, the effect of Na-In alloy thickness were studied by treating Na metal anode with low (0.1&nbsp;M) concentration and high concentration (0.25&nbsp;M) of indium chloride solution in tetrahydrofuran solvent. The concentration of metal chloride solution greatly influences the thickness and surface flatness of Na-In alloy layer. Na-In metal anode prepared with low and high concentrations (0.1&nbsp;M and 0.25&nbsp;M) exhibits high overpotential, uneven plating/stripping, poor cycle life, and instability than Na-In metal anode prepared with 0.15&nbsp;M indium chloride solution (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) The optimized concentration of 0.15&nbsp;M metal chloride-based electrode demonstrates excellent cyclability, lower overpotential, and dendrite free Na deposition than other concentrations. The low concentration metal chloride solution would result in the formation of unstable and very thin Na-M artificial SEI layer, while high concentration of metal chloride would result in thick Na-M artificial SEI layer over the Na metal, influencing the interphase resistance of metal anode. The Nyquist plots (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) of Na-In anode prepared with 0.15&nbsp;M concentration exhibited lower charge transfer resistance and better interphase properties than Na-In metal anode prepared with other concentrations (0.1&nbsp;M and 0.25&nbsp;M). When the Na metal is treated with low concentration of metal chloride solution, the formation of Na-M alloy layer and NaCl layer would be incomplete, leading to unfavorable interface while at high concentration of metal chloride solution, excess of metal chloride salts and NaCl could increase the interface resistance. Also, Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) compares the performance of the several protective layer over previously studied alloy layer, and observed that the current protective alloy layers are showing the high performance than other protective techniques.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202300135-sec-0110">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202300135-sec-0110-title">3.3 SEI Layer Formation</h3>
            
            <p>The components of SEI layer, and the change in components of the SEI layer was studied by XPS, to analyze the stability of the Na anode protected with Na-In alloy after cycling (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0004">4</a>). We focused on the In3d, Na1s, Cl2p, O1s, and C1s XPS profiles, because Na-In, NaCl and other organic components are one of the key components of the SEI layer. After 5th cycle, the deconvoluted In 3d spectrum at 446.27&nbsp;eV (In 3d<sub>5/2</sub>) and 453.36&nbsp;eV (In 3d<sub>3/2</sub>) in (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0004">4a</a>) shows the predominant contribution from Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy layer with a minor contribution from In metal and In<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> before cycling, indicates the presence of mechanical stable alloy type artificial interface layer over Na anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0044_R_d62996621e1305" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Vac. Sci. Technol. A">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na1s spectrum in (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0004">4b</a>) shows a broad curve at ≈1072&nbsp;eV due to contributions from NaCl and Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> phases. The two peaks at 198.2 and 200.3&nbsp;eV in the Cl2p spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0004">4c</a>) were designated to Cl2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Cl2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, indicating the presence of Na-rich NaCl layer over the Na anode protected with Na-In alloy, helping the facile Na deposition and stripping process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0002_R_d62996621e1326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">2</a>, <a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0045_R_d62996621e1326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">45</a>, <a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0046_R_d62996621e1326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C1s and O1s spectrum of Na anode protected Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) shows the emergence of new organic SEI components for sodium alkoxides after cycling process. The deconvoluted C1s spectra and O1s spectrum after cycling shows the emergence of new functionalities (C<span class="icomoon"></span>H, C<span class="icomoon"></span>O, Na<span class="icomoon"></span>O) over Na anode protected with Na-In alloy. This is mainly attributed to slight reduction of liquid electrolyte over Na-In alloy during initial cycling, helping to form components of natural SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0011_R_d62996621e1339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Cent. Sci.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The origin of the organic components plays a very crucial to provide flexibility to the multifunctional SEI layer at high current and high-capacity conditions. However, the signals of NaCl layer from Cl2p and Na1s spectrum decreased with etching, and completely disappeared after etching process, indicating that the top layer of alloy anode is covered by Na-rich NaCl phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0047_R_d62996621e1344" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na-ion gets diffused through this Na-rich NaCl and get deposited beneath the alloy layer. The Na-rich NaCl component establishes the necessary potential gradient to drive Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion through the alloy layer. Benefiting from the thinner NaCl layer with high elastic modulus, and high electronic resistivity, the hybrid interface can withstand long repeated plating and stripping process. The deconvoluted In3d, Na1s, Cl2p XPS spectrum of Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy protected anode after 25th cycle (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0005">5</a>) shows the similar pattern as 5th cycle, indicating the presence of robust and stable artificial SEI layer over Na anode that regulates the Na deposition/stripping through facile Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion flux.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0048_R_d62996621e1363" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Mater. Lett.">48</a>, <a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0049_R_d62996621e1363" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Sci. Adv.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electronically resistive NaCl layer over metal anodes are highly insoluble in organic electrolytes and will hamper electrolyte decomposition on continuous cycling. The formation of ultrathin Na-rich NaCl layer over Na metal anodes plays a dominant role in stabilizing the metal anode. Similar SEI formation trend is noted for other alloy protected anodes as shown in (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8–S10</a>, Supporting Information) and the difference being the type of metal alloy layer over the Na anode.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202300135-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b860b91a-fa7b-4fe0-b1df-e541cb378df1/adfm202300135-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b860b91a-fa7b-4fe0-b1df-e541cb378df1/adfm202300135-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/738c2e63-5b59-4583-aadd-f9071395becd/adfm202300135-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b860b91a-fa7b-4fe0-b1df-e541cb378df1/adfm202300135-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202300135-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202300135" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEI layer formation over Na anode protected with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy after 5th cycle, a) high resolution In3d spectrum b) high resolution Na1s spectrum, and c) high resolution Cl2p spectrum</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202300135-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c7bcf2a6-f756-408e-a6d0-a59f1e2d7c91/adfm202300135-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c7bcf2a6-f756-408e-a6d0-a59f1e2d7c91/adfm202300135-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/48d49b83-1471-405a-a4b3-5bd8c7ad795f/adfm202300135-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c7bcf2a6-f756-408e-a6d0-a59f1e2d7c91/adfm202300135-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202300135-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202300135" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEI layer formation over Na anode protected with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy after 5th and 25th cycle, a) high resolution In3d spectrum b) high resolution Na1s spectrum, and c) high resolution Cl2p spectrum</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To understand the origin of the superior performance of Na anode with alloy interface, ex-situ SEM analysis were performed on Pristine Na metal and Na metal with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1,</sub> Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>1</sub>Zn<sub>13</sub> Na<sub>1</sub>Bi<sub>1</sub> interface after cycling (100 cycles). The SEM images of the pristine Na anode (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a,b</a>, Supporting Information) revealed a rough surface along with uneven Na deposition, indicating a severe dendrite formation. Such a rough and uneven surface mainly originated from the repeated inhomogeneous Na deposition in host less Na anode with unstable SEI layer. In contrast, the SEM images of Na anode with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1,</sub> Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>1</sub>Zn<sub>13</sub>, Na<sub>1</sub>Bi<sub>1</sub> interface exhibited a smooth surface without any signs of sodium dendrites as shown in (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11c–f</a>, Supporting Information). The SEM elemental mapping analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) of Na-In shows, even after cycling (100 cycles), SEI components (Na, In, Cl) over the Na-In protective layer still remains intact, confirming the mechanical integrity of the alloy anode. This indicates the effective protective of Na anode with hybrid SEI layer comprising Na-rich components and alloy type interface layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0050_R_d62996621e1467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202300135-sec-0120">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202300135-sec-0120-title">3.4 Kinetics of Na Metal Deposition/Stripping</h3>
            
            <p>To understand the kinetics of the Na deposition/stripping at high current conditions, the stability of pristine Na anode and Na-In protected Na anode were evaluated at current density of 5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. The charge discharge profile in (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0006">6a</a>) shows that pristine Na metal has a large overpotential due to resistance in mass transfer at high current conditions, and the overpotential increased with cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0039_R_d62996621e1489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Acc. Chem. Res.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The bare Na metal anode exhibits unstable behavior after 80&nbsp;h, much lower than the performance at low current rates. However, Na anode with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> interface exhibited a stable voltage profile with a very low overpotential (19&nbsp;mV), lower than pristine Na metal. Unlike pristine Na anode, the overpotential of Na anode with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> interface remains same throughout the cycling, and can be cycled efficiently over 266&nbsp;h. The absence of a voltage drop and voltage fluctuations indicated the presence of a stable interface for Na deposition and low nucleation overpotential to achieve enhanced kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0051_R_d62996621e1503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Nat. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202300135-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/91e506e6-4d6c-4a43-b57c-f38e7ef8b51a/adfm202300135-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/91e506e6-4d6c-4a43-b57c-f38e7ef8b51a/adfm202300135-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d109a239-1bd5-4e63-8047-e21a105c9a95/adfm202300135-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/91e506e6-4d6c-4a43-b57c-f38e7ef8b51a/adfm202300135-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202300135-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202300135" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of Na anode and Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> protected anode at different conditions a) 5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and capacity of 2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, b) 2 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and capacity of 5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, and Nyquist plots of different anodes c) before cycling, and d) after 75 cycles</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The cyclic stability of pristine Na anode and Na anode with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> anode is studied in high cell capacity of 5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and current density of 2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, and the data is presented in (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0006">6b</a>). The pristine sodium anode exhibited uneven plating and stripping after 90&nbsp;h of cycling with a high overpotential of ≈50&nbsp;mV. At high capacity testing, sodium metal could settle at a few local spots as dead Na during cycling, which created large grooves and dendrite throughout the stripping process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0052_R_d62996621e1555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Joule">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na anode with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> layer performs efficient plating and stripping performances without dendrite formation for over 295&nbsp;h with low over potential. Since the Na deposition occurred beneath the Na-rich SEI layer, the volume expansion associated with high-capacity Na plating would exert high outward pressure on the SEI layer than low capacity testing. Such stress will lead to breakage of SEI layer at a threshold limit, and lead to fast electrolyte consumption, dendrite formation and early cell failure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0042_R_d62996621e1564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Electrochim. Acta">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>Such irregular Na plating/stripping in pristine Na anode generated pressure on the SEI layer followed by the breakage of SEI layer. However, a strong tensile strength from Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> layer and flexibility from Na-rich NaCl layer/organic SEI layer helped to withstand the stress and volume expansion exerted during high capacity storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0053_R_d62996621e1574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Mater. Sci.">53</a>, <a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0054_R_d62996621e1574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Phys. Chem. Solids">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, Na anode with Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>2</sub> interface exhibited even plating and stripping even after 290&nbsp;h with low overpotential of 21&nbsp;mV, depicting the role of artificial interface layer for achieving high-capacity Na metal anodes (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). However, Na anode with Na<sub>1</sub>Zn<sub>13</sub> Na<sub>1</sub>Bi<sub>1</sub> alloy layers cannot be cycled efficiently over 100&nbsp;h due to poor mechanical strength of the alloy layers. Nanoindentation studies were carried out using a commercial atomic force microscope to determine the Young's modulus of Na metal and Na-In alloy anode. The binary intermetallic compounds (Na-M) generally have higher mechanical stability than pristine Na metal. The results in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information) shows that the Young's modulus of NaIn alloy is ≈8&nbsp;Gpa, twice higher than pristine Na metal (≈4&nbsp;Gpa), which is consistent with the claims made in the manuscript. The corresponding average Young's modulus value (32*32 matrix, Hertz model) of Na metal and NaIn alloy is ≈2.2&nbsp;Gpa, and ≈4.2&nbsp;Gpa respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0054_R_d62996621e1602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Phys. Chem. Solids">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, we conclude that the hybrid multifunctional SEI layer comprising mechanically strong alloy component–Na rich NaCl component–organic component improved the cell performance at high current rates and high capacity storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0035_R_d62996621e1607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Materialia">35</a>, <a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0015_R_d62996621e1607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Mater. Chem. A">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>To elucidate the effect of the alloy interface layer relative to pristine sodium, we conducted EIS testing. The Nyquist plots of the Na metal anode with interface layer and pristine Na are shown in (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0006">6c,d</a>). The first semicircle in the high-frequency regions of the plots of both pristine Na metal and Na metal alloy interface layer electrodes show the resistance of the SEI layer. The second semicircle in the low-frequency region indicates the charge transfer resistance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0055_R_d62996621e1617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Energy Storage Mater.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The charge transfer resistance of pristine Na anode before cycling was low due to native formed SEI layer after reaction of Na metal with electrolyte. However, after cycling (75 cycles), the interface resistance of pristine Na anode increased significantly (R<sub>ct</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;17.4&nbsp;Ω) as Na metal undergoes continuous unwanted side reactions with the electrolyte, resulting in the accumulation of thicker, and poor Na-ion conductive SEI layer. The increasing charge transfer resistance in pristine Na metal anode impedes the kinetics of the Na deposition/stripping process, leading to failure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0030_R_d62996621e1624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Energy Storage Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The Nyquist plots of Na anode with alloy protective alloy interface shows a higher charge transfer resistance before cycling, due to higher electrical resistivity from originating from metal-alloy interface than pristine Na metal. The interface resistance of Na-M alloys before cycling increases in the following order (Na-Zn&lt; Na-Bi&lt;Na-Sn&lt;Na-In). However, after 75 cycles, the surface of Na anode with protective alloy interface stabilized resulting in reduction of interface resistance. The interface resistance of Na-In, Na-Sn, Na-Zn, and Na-Bi alloys were 2.78&nbsp;Ω, 2.82&nbsp;Ω, 3.2&nbsp;Ω, and 3.4&nbsp;Ω respectively. This is mainly attributed to stabilization of artificial interface by formation new natural SEI layer after the reaction between Na-M alloy and organic liquid electrolyte. The formation of organic SEI layer components during the initial plating/stripping process increased the interface contact between Na metal–alloy layer–artificial/natural SEI components, thereby reducing any voids or defects in interface layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0056_R_d62996621e1632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Adv. Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>The origin of new polar functional groups over the artificial SEI layer after initial cycling can facilitate easy and rapid Na-ion transfer kinetics from electrolyte ions and deposit them below the alloy interface. The small interface resistance in alloy protected Na anode was mainly attributable to the conformal organic and in organic SEI layer that could enhance the interfacial contact between components of the anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0057_R_d62996621e1637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Nano Energy">57</a>, <a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0033_R_d62996621e1637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Faster ionic conduction and transportation close to the electrode surface is favorable for achieving low charge transfer resistance, enabling the alloy protected metal anode perform efficiently at high current and high capacity testing conditions. The low and stable interface resistance in Na anode with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> and Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>2</sub> enhanced the Na deposition/stripping kinetics and interfacial stability than pristine Na metal and Na<sub>1</sub>Zn<sub>13</sub>/Na<sub>1</sub>Bi<sub>1</sub> protected Na metal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0045_R_d62996621e1660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202300135-sec-0130">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202300135-sec-0130-title">3.5 Density Functional Theory Calculations</h3>
            
            <p>We have performed density functional theory (DFT) calculations to understand the origin of the excellent performance of Na anode with alloy protective layer over pristine Na anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0058_R_d62996621e1673" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Engineering">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>The surface energy and diffusion barrier required for the Na adatom to diffuse across the surface of interlayer were evaluated for Na anode with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub>, and Na<sub>1</sub>Bi<sub>1,</sub> NaSn<sub>2</sub> and NaZn<sub>13</sub> alloy interface layers.(<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0007">7a</a>) shows the evaluated adsorption energies of the Na, NaIn, and NaBi, NaSn<sub>2</sub> and NaZn<sub>13</sub> alloys on the corresponding (100) and (111) planes. Among them, the Na adatoms yielded higher surface energy (0.369&nbsp;eV and 0.34) than other alloys. The surface energy levels of the other four alloys, namely NaIn (100 and 111), NaBi (100 and 111), NaSn<sub>2</sub>(100 and 111), NaZn<sub>13</sub>(100 and 111) were (0.021&nbsp;eV and −0.16&nbsp;eV), (−0.361&nbsp;eV and −0.56&nbsp;eV), (0.082 &amp; −0.133 and) and (−0.645 and −0.731) respectively, making them the favorable candidates with minimum surface energy for Na adsorption. The lower surface energy of the alloys (NaIn, NaBi, NaSn<sub>2</sub>, and NaZn<sub>13</sub>) relative to that of Na metal clearly indicates that low surface energy is the key factor for facilitating ion diffusion across the surface; and the sodium deposition is inclined toward the alloy surface. The diffusion barrier of Na metal is 0.27&nbsp;eV, which is excessive for inducing dendrite formation on its surface. As depicted in (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0007">7b,c</a>), the diffusion barriers of NaIn, NaBi, NaSn<sub>2</sub> and NaZn<sub>13</sub> are 0.188&nbsp;eV, 0.475&nbsp;eV, 0.13&nbsp;eV, and 0.335&nbsp;eV on the (100) plane, and 0.278&nbsp;eV, 0.368&nbsp;eV, 0.451&nbsp;eV, and 0.301&nbsp;eV on the (111) plane, respectively. As shown in (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0007">7d</a>) atoms arrangement and diffusion pathway for each alloy layers in corresponding planes of (100) &amp; (111). respectively. In which green colored arrows show the different pathways between different adsorption sites. Additionally, the activation energy barrier of pristine Na anode and alloy protected Na anode in different planes (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) confirms that diffusion kinetics are inclined toward the favorable planes of the alloy, helping to achieve a rapid kinetics in Na anode protected with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0030_R_d62996621e1729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Energy Storage Mater.">30</a>, <a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0059_R_d62996621e1729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Energy Environ. Sci.">59</a>, <a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0060_R_d62996621e1729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Sci. Adv.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202300135-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/08921f28-9429-4b37-8a10-23daabec00ff/adfm202300135-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/08921f28-9429-4b37-8a10-23daabec00ff/adfm202300135-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/53ce2c47-2e7e-4a6d-b5c9-af61a386cfd9/adfm202300135-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/08921f28-9429-4b37-8a10-23daabec00ff/adfm202300135-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202300135-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202300135" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Na adsorption energy and diffusion activation barrier for NaIn, NaSn<sub>2</sub>, NaZn<sub>13</sub>, and NaBi. The activation barrier for (100) plane is shown in the left panel of b), and the activation barrier for (111) plane is shown in the right panel of. c), d) the Na diffusion pathway of each alloy. The green arrows indicate the diffusion pathway.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202300135-sec-0140">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202300135-sec-0140-title">3.6 Full Cell Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>Considering the potential practical applications of metal anodes, we assembled a full cell using Na anode with/without Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> layer and coupled with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode. The synthesis procedure and properties of NVP cathode used were studied in our previous work.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202300135-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202300135-bib-0061_R_d62996621e1779" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cells are cycled in the large voltage window (1.0–3.8&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) to extract more Na<sup>+</sup> ions out of NVP cathode and insert more Na<sup>+</sup> ions into the metal anode. The charge discharge profile of the full cell shows two stable plateaus, corresponding to reversible reaction between NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>4</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phases (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0008">8a</a>). The discharge capacity of full-cell at 0.1, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 C was 161, 157, 155, 151, and 150&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>(Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0008">8b</a>) respectively. The capacity obtained with Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> anode is much higher than pristine Na at all C rates, and also, they exhibited a low overpotential than pristine Na metal anode. Moreover, the full-cell will Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> layer displayed a stable charge-discharge behavior upon cycling. These results further confirm that the hybrid multifunctional interfaces aided the formation of a stable SEI layer over Na metal anode, thereby inhibiting the unwanted consumption of the electrolyte. In contrast, the cell with pristine Na metal anode shows poor cyclic stability and highly fluctuating columbic efficiency due to unwanted electrolyte reaction from highly reactive Na metal (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202300135-fig-0008">8c</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202300135-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4768ce26-f8ea-497d-94d7-e74288c1cf37/adfm202300135-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4768ce26-f8ea-497d-94d7-e74288c1cf37/adfm202300135-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5c0c5e6c-133a-482d-bca5-f0385cc0aa64/adfm202300135-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4768ce26-f8ea-497d-94d7-e74288c1cf37/adfm202300135-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202300135-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202300135" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of Na-Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub>// Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full-cell: a) Charge-discharge profiles at different rates, b) rate performance comparison with pristine Na anode, and c) cyclic stability at 1C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202300135-sec-0150">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202300135-sec-0150-title">4 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have demonstrated that a Series of hybrid multifunctional interfaces (Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub>, Na<sub>1</sub>Sn<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>1</sub>Zn<sub>13</sub> Na<sub>1</sub>Bi<sub>1</sub>) made by a simple self-alloying reaction as artificial SEI layer for providing stable interface in Na metal anode. The surface energy of the protective alloys relative to bare Na metal is the key factor for facilitating facile ion diffusion across the interface. The hybrid Na-ion conducting organic-inorganic SEI components can facilitate uniform Na deposition by regulating the Na-ion flux with a low overpotential than pristine Na metal. The mechanically strong Na-In alloy with Na rich NaCl phase can efficiently drive large deposition (10&nbsp;mAh) of Na metal under the protective alloys. Moreover, the hybrid SEI provides a highly flexible and dense membrane to accommodates the volume changes, regulate the Na<sup>+</sup> ion flux, and reduces the local current density to achieve efficient Na deposition at high current (5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) and withstand repeated Na deposition and stripping process for ≈800&nbsp;h. The lower surface energy of Na<sub>1</sub>In<sub>1</sub> alloys relative to that of Na metal and Na<sub>1</sub>Bi<sub>1</sub> alloy facilitates facile ion diffusion across the surface; and the deposition is inclined toward the alloy surface. Our finding is significant that it provides facile surface stabilization strategy for dendrite free Na metal anode, and opens up a promising research direction for development of high energy density sodium metal batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
